the Lake District is one of the few places in England where red squirrels have a sizeable population.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fell_pony.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Fell_pony.jpg/170px-Fell_pony.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Fell_pony.jpg/255px-Fell_pony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Fell_pony.jpg/340px-Fell_pony.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fell_pony.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wild <a href="/wiki/Fell_pony" title="Fell pony">Fell pony</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Fells" class="mw-redirect" title="Fells">fells</a>, native to <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West England</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Lake District is home to a great variety of wildlife, due to its range of varied topography, lakes and forests. It provides a home for the <a href="/wiki/Red_squirrel" title="Red squirrel">red squirrel</a> and colonies of <a href="/wiki/Sundew" class="mw-redirect" title="Sundew">sundew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butterwort" class="mw-redirect" title="Butterwort">butterwort</a>, two of the few <a href="/wiki/Carnivorous_plant" title="Carnivorous plant">carnivorous plants</a> native to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a>. The Lake District is a major sanctuary for the red squirrel and has the largest population in England (out of the estimated 140,000 red squirrels in the United Kingdom, compared with about 2.5&#160;million <a href="/wiki/Eastern_gray_squirrel" title="Eastern gray squirrel">grey squirrels</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Lake District is home to a range of bird species,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">RSPB</a> maintain a reserve in Haweswater.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> England\'s only nesting pair of <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a> can be found in the Lake District. The female golden eagle has not been seen since 2004 although the male still remains.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Conservationists believe he is now the only resident golden eagle in England.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Following recolonisation attempts, a pair of <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">ospreys</a> nested in the Lake District for the first time in over 150 years near <a href="/wiki/Bassenthwaite_Lake" title="Bassenthwaite Lake">Bassenthwaite Lake</a> during 2001. Ospreys now frequently migrate north from <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> in the spring to nest in the Lake District, and a total of 23 chicks have fledged in The Lakes since 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Another bird species which has had recolonisation attempts is the <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kite</a> which, as of 2012, have a population of approximately 90 in the dense forest areas near <a href="/wiki/Grizedale_Forest" title="Grizedale Forest">Grizedale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Conservationists hope the reintroduction will create a large red kite population in the Lake District and in <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West England</a> where the red kite population is low.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Other bird species resident to the Lake District include the <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">buzzard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dipper" title="Dipper">dipper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raven" title="Raven">raven</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Seasonal birds include the <a href="/wiki/Ring_ouzel" title="Ring ouzel">ring ouzel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Redstart" title="Redstart">redstart</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The lakes of the Lake District support three rare and endangered species of fish: the <a href="/wiki/Coregonus_vandesius" title="Coregonus vandesius">vendace</a>, which can be found only in Derwent Water and until 2008 in <a href="/wiki/Bassenthwaite_Lake" title="Bassenthwaite Lake">Bassenthwaite Lake</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Vendace have struggled in recent years with naturally occurring algae becoming a threat and the lakes gradually getting warmer.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\nVendace have been moved to higher lakes on a number of occasions to preserve the species, notably in 2005 and 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The Lakes are also home to two other rare species: the <a href="/wiki/Schelly" title="Schelly">schelly</a>, which lives in Brothers Water, Haweswater, Red Tarn and Ullswater, and the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_char" title="Arctic char">Arctic charr</a>, which can be found in Buttermere, Coniston Water, Crummock Water, Ennerdale Water, Haweswater, Loweswater, Thirlmere, Wast Water, and Windermere.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CoregonusVandesius.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/CoregonusVandesius.jpg/220px-CoregonusVandesius.jpg" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/CoregonusVandesius.jpg/330px-CoregonusVandesius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/CoregonusVandesius.jpg/440px-CoregonusVandesius.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1077" data-file-height="544" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CoregonusVandesius.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The vendace (<i><a href="/wiki/Coregonus_vandesius" title="Coregonus vandesius">Coregonus vandesius</a></i>) is England\'s rarest species of fish, and is found only in the Lake District.</div></div></div>\n<p>In recent years, some important changes have been made to fisheries byelaws covering the north west region of England, to help protect some of the rarest fish species. In 2002, the <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">Environment Agency</a> introduced a new fisheries byelaw, banning the use of all freshwater fish as live or dead bait in 14 of the lakes in the Lake District. Anglers not complying with the new byelaw can face fines of up to \xc2\xa32,500. There are 14 lakes in the Lake District which are affected. These are: Bassenthwaite Lake, Brothers Water, Buttermere, Coniston Water, Crummock Water, Derwent Water, Ennerdale Water, Haweswater, Loweswater, Red Tarn, Thirlmere, Ullswater, Wast Water and Windermere.\n</p><p>The lakes and waters of the Lake District do not naturally support as many species of fish as other similar habitats in the south of the country and elsewhere in Europe. Some fish that do thrive there are particularly at risk from introduction of new species.\n</p><p>The introduction of non-native fish can lead to the predation of the native fish fauna or competition for food. There is also the risk of disease being introduced, which can further threaten native populations. In some cases, the introduced species can disturb the environment so much that it becomes unsuitable for particular fish. For example, a major problem has been found with <a href="/wiki/Ruffe" title="Ruffe">ruffe</a>. This non-native fish has now been introduced into a number of lakes in recent years. It is known that ruffe eat the eggs of vendace, which are particularly vulnerable because of their long incubation period. This means that they are susceptible to predators for up to 120 days. The eggs of other fish, for example <a href="/wiki/Rutilus" title="Rutilus">roach</a>, are only at risk for as little as three days.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture_and_forestry">Agriculture and forestry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Agriculture and forestry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Forestry_on_Harter_Fell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Forestry_on_Harter_Fell.jpg/220px-Forestry_on_Harter_Fell.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Forestry_on_Harter_Fell.jpg/330px-Forestry_on_Harter_Fell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Forestry_on_Harter_Fell.jpg/440px-Forestry_on_Harter_Fell.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Forestry_on_Harter_Fell.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Forestry operations on <a href="/wiki/Harter_Fell,_Eskdale" class="mw-redirect" title="Harter Fell, Eskdale">Harter Fell</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Farming, and in particular <a href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic sheep">sheep farming</a>, has been the major industry in the region since <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman</a> times. The breed most closely associated with the area is the tough <a href="/wiki/Herdwick" title="Herdwick">Herdwick</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Rough_Fell" title="Rough Fell">Rough Fell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swaledale_(sheep)" class="mw-redirect" title="Swaledale (sheep)">Swaledale</a> sheep also common. Sheep farming remains important both for the economy of the region and for preserving the landscape which visitors want to see. Features such as <a href="/wiki/Dry_stone_wall" class="mw-redirect" title="Dry stone wall">dry stone walls</a>, for example, are there as a result of sheep farming. Some land is also used for <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">dairy farming</a>.\n</p><p>The area was badly affected by the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Foot-and-mouth_disease" title="Foot-and-mouth disease">foot-and-mouth disease</a> across the United Kingdom in 2001. The outbreak started in <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a> in February, but had spread to <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a> by end of March.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Thousands of sheep, include the native <a href="/wiki/Herdwick" title="Herdwick">Herdwicks</a> which graze on the fellsides across the district, were destroyed. In replacing the sheep, one problem to overcome was that many of the lost sheep were <i>heafed</i>, that is, they knew their part of the unfenced fell and did not stray, with this knowledge being passed between generations. With all the sheep lost at once, this knowledge has to be relearnt and some of the fells have had discreet <a href="/wiki/Electric_fence" title="Electric fence">electric fences</a> strung across them for a period of five years, to allow the sheep to "re-heaf".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> At the time of the outbreak, worries existed about the future of certain species of sheep such as <a href="/wiki/Ryeland" title="Ryeland">Ryeland</a> and Herdwick in the district,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> however these fears have been allayed and sheep now occupy the district in abundance.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thirleme_069.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Thirleme_069.jpg/220px-Thirleme_069.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Thirleme_069.jpg/330px-Thirleme_069.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Thirleme_069.jpg/440px-Thirleme_069.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thirleme_069.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Herdwick" title="Herdwick">Herdwick</a> grazing above <a href="/wiki/Thirlmere" title="Thirlmere">Thirlmere</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Forestry has also assumed greater importance over the course of the last century with the establishment of extensive conifer plantations around <a href="/wiki/Whinlatter_Pass" title="Whinlatter Pass">Whinlatter Pass</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Ennerdale_Water" title="Ennerdale Water">Ennerdale</a> and at <a href="/wiki/Grizedale_Forest" title="Grizedale Forest">Grizedale Forest</a> amongst other places. There are extensive plantations of non-native pine trees.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>With its wealth of rock types and their abundance in the landscape, mining and quarrying have long been significant activities in the Lake District economy. In <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> times, the Lake District was a major source of stone <a href="/wiki/Axe" title="Axe">axes</a>, examples of which have been found all over Britain. The primary site, on the slopes of the Langdale Pikes, is sometimes described as a "stone axe factory" of the <a href="/wiki/Langdale_axe_industry" title="Langdale axe industry">Langdale axe industry</a>. Some of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Stone_circle" title="Stone circle">stone circles</a> in Britain are connected with this industry.\n</p><p>Mining, particularly of copper, lead (often associated with quantities of silver), <a href="/wiki/Baryte" title="Baryte">baryte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slate" title="Slate">slate</a>, was historically a major Lakeland industry, mainly from the 16th to 19th centuries. Coppiced woodland was used extensively to provide <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> for smelting. Some mining still takes place today; for example, slate mining continues at the <a href="/wiki/Honister_Slate_Mine" title="Honister Slate Mine">Honister Mines</a>, at the top of <a href="/wiki/Honister_Pass" title="Honister Pass">Honister Pass</a>. Abandoned mine workings can be found on fellsides throughout the district. The locally mined graphite led to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Pencil" title="Pencil">pencil</a> industry, especially around <a href="/wiki/Keswick,_Cumbria" title="Keswick, Cumbria">Keswick</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lake_District_near_Torver.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Lake_District_near_Torver.jpg/220px-Lake_District_near_Torver.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Lake_District_near_Torver.jpg/330px-Lake_District_near_Torver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Lake_District_near_Torver.jpg/440px-Lake_District_near_Torver.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lake_District_near_Torver.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical Lake District scene</div></div></div>\n<p>In the middle of the 19th century, half the world textile industry\'s <a href="/wiki/Bobbin" title="Bobbin">bobbin</a> supply came from the Lake District area. Over the past century, however, tourism has grown rapidly to become the area\'s primary source of income.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_tourism">Development of tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Development of tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lake_District&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(September 2011)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Early visitors to the Lake District, who travelled for the education and pleasure of the journey, include <a href="/wiki/Celia_Fiennes" title="Celia Fiennes">Celia Fiennes</a> who in 1698 undertook a journey the length of England, including riding through <a href="/wiki/Kendal" title="Kendal">Kendal</a> and over <a href="/wiki/Kirkstone_Pass" title="Kirkstone Pass">Kirkstone Pass</a> into <a href="/wiki/Patterdale" title="Patterdale">Patterdale</a>. Her experiences and impressions were published in her book <i>Great Journey to Newcastle and Cornwall</i>:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>As I walked down at this place I was walled on both sides by those inaccessible high rocky barren hills which hang over one\xe2\x80\x99s head in some places and appear very terrible; and from them springs many little currents of water from the sides and clefts which trickle down to some lower part where it runs swiftly over the stones and shelves in the way, which makes a pleasant rush and murmuring noise and like a snowball is increased by each spring trickling down on either side of those hills, and so descends into the bottoms which are a Moorish ground in which in many places the waters stand, and so form some of those Lakes as it did here.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>In 1724, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">Daniel Defoe</a> published the first volume of <i>A Tour Thro\' the Whole Island of Great Britain</i>. He commented on <a href="/wiki/Westmorland" title="Westmorland">Westmorland</a> that it was:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>the wildest, most barren and frightful of any that I have passed over in England, or even Wales itself; the west side, which borders on Cumberland, is indeed bounded by a chain of almost unpassable mountains which, in the language of the country, are called fells.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Towards the end of the 18th century, the area was becoming more popular with travellers. This was partly a result of wars in <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a>, restricting the possibility of travel there. In 1778 <a href="/wiki/Thomas_West_(priest)" title="Thomas West (priest)">Thomas West</a> produced <i>A Guide to the Lakes</i>, which began the era of modern tourism.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Claife_Station.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Claife_Station.jpg/220px-Claife_Station.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Claife_Station.jpg/330px-Claife_Station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Claife_Station.jpg/440px-Claife_Station.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Claife_Station.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Claife Station on the western shore of <a href="/wiki/Windermere" title="Windermere">Windermere</a></div></div></div>\n<p>West listed "stations", viewpoints where tourists could enjoy the best views of the landscape, being encouraged to appreciated the formal qualities of the landscape and to apply aesthetic values. At some of these stations, buildings were erected to help this process. The remains of Claife Station (on the western shore <a href="/wiki/Windermere" title="Windermere">Windermere</a> below <a href="/wiki/Claife_Heights" title="Claife Heights">Claife Heights</a>) can be visited today.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a> published his <i>Guide to the Lakes</i> in 1810, and by 1835 it had reached its fifth edition, now called <i>A Guide Through the District of the Lakes in the North of England</i>. This book was particularly influential in popularising the region. Wordsworth\'s favourite valley was Dunnerdale or the <a href="/wiki/Duddon_Valley" title="Duddon Valley">Duddon Valley</a> nestling in the south west of the Lake District.\n</p><p>The railways led to another expansion in tourism. The <a href="/wiki/Kendal_and_Windermere_Railway" title="Kendal and Windermere Railway">Kendal and Windermere Railway</a> was the first to penetrate the Lake District, reaching <a href="/wiki/Kendal" title="Kendal">Kendal</a> in 1846 and <a href="/wiki/Windermere,_Cumbria_(town)" title="Windermere, Cumbria (town)">Windermere</a> in 1847. The line to <a href="/wiki/Coniston,_Cumbria" title="Coniston, Cumbria">Coniston</a> opened in 1848 (although until 1857 this was only linked to the national network with ferries between <a href="/wiki/Fleetwood" title="Fleetwood">Fleetwood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barrow-in-Furness" title="Barrow-in-Furness">Barrow-in-Furness</a>); the line from <a href="/wiki/Penrith,_Cumbria" title="Penrith, Cumbria">Penrith</a> through <a href="/wiki/Keswick,_Cumbria" title="Keswick, Cumbria">Keswick</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cockermouth" title="Cockermouth">Cockermouth</a> in 1865; and the line to <a href="/wiki/Lakeside,_Cumbria" title="Lakeside, Cumbria">Lakeside</a> at the foot of <a href="/wiki/Windermere" title="Windermere">Windermere</a> in 1869. The railways, built with traditional industry in mind, brought with them a huge increase in the number of visitors, thus contributing to the growth of the tourism industry. Railway services were supplemented by steamer boats on the major lakes of <a href="/wiki/Ullswater" title="Ullswater">Ullswater</a>, Windermere, <a href="/wiki/Coniston_Water" title="Coniston Water">Coniston Water</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Derwent_Water" class="mw-redirect" title="Derwent Water">Derwent Water</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:STEAMER_ON_ULLSWATER.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/STEAMER_ON_ULLSWATER.jpg/220px-STEAMER_ON_ULLSWATER.jpg" width="220" height="106" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/STEAMER_ON_ULLSWATER.jpg/330px-STEAMER_ON_ULLSWATER.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/STEAMER_ON_ULLSWATER.jpg/440px-STEAMER_ON_ULLSWATER.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1528" data-file-height="736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:STEAMER_ON_ULLSWATER.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A steamer on <a href="/wiki/Ullswater" title="Ullswater">Ullswater</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The growth in tourist numbers continued into the age of the motor car, when railways began to be closed or run down. The formation of the <a href="/wiki/Lake_District_National_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake District National Park">Lake District National Park</a> in 1951 recognised the need to protect the Lake District environment from excessive commercial or industrial exploitation, preserving that which visitors come to see, without any restriction on the movement of people into and around the district. The <a href="/wiki/M6_Motorway" class="mw-redirect" title="M6 Motorway">M6 Motorway</a> helped bring traffic to the Lake District, passing up its eastern flank. The narrow roads present a challenge for traffic flow and, from the 1960s, certain areas have been very congested.\n</p><p>Whilst the roads and railways provided easier access to the area, many people were drawn to Lakeland by the publication of the <i><a href="/wiki/Pictorial_Guide_to_the_Lakeland_Fells" title="Pictorial Guide to the Lakeland Fells">Pictorial Guide to the Lakeland Fells</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Wainwright" title="Alfred Wainwright">Alfred Wainwright</a>. First published between 1955 and 1966, these books provided detailed information on 214 fells across the region, with carefully hand-drawn maps and panoramas, and also stories and asides which add to the colour of the area. They are still used by many visitors to the area as guides for walking excursions, with the ultimate goal of <a href="/wiki/Peak_bagging" title="Peak bagging">bagging</a> the complete list of <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_Wainwrights" title="List of Wainwrights">Wainwrights</a></i>. The famous guides were revised by <a href="/wiki/Chris_Jesty" title="Chris Jesty">Chris Jesty</a> between 2005 and 2009 to reflect changes, mainly in valley access and paths, and are currently being revised by Clive Hutchby, the author of The Wainwright Companion. The first of the revised volumes, Book One: The Eastern Fells, was published in March 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the early 1960s, the National Park Authority has employed rangers to help cope with increasing tourism and development, the first being <a href="/wiki/John_Wyatt_(writer)" title="John Wyatt (writer)">John Wyatt</a>, who has since written a number of guide books. He was joined two years later by a second, and since then the number of rangers has been rising.\n</p><p>The area has also become associated with writer <a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a>. A number of tourists visit to see her family home, with particularly large numbers coming from Japan.\n</p><p>Tourism has now become the park\'s major industry, with about 12&#160;million visitors each year, mainly from the UK\'s larger settlements, China, Japan, Spain, Germany and the US.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Windermere Lake Steamers are Cumbria\'s most popular charging tourist attraction with about 1.35&#160;million paying customers each year, and the local economy is dependent upon tourists. The negative impact of tourism has been seen, however. <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">Soil erosion</a>, caused by walking, is now a significant problem, with millions of pounds being spent to protect overused paths. In 2006, two <a href="/wiki/Visitor_center" title="Visitor center">tourist information centres</a> in the National Park were closed.\n</p><p>Cultural tourism is becoming an increasingly important part of the wider tourist industry. The Lake District\'s links with a wealth of artists and writers and its strong history of providing summer theatre performances in the old Blue Box of Century Theatre are strong attractions for visiting tourists. The tradition of theatre is carried on by venues such as <a href="/wiki/Theatre_by_the_Lake" title="Theatre by the Lake">Theatre by the Lake</a> in Keswick with its summer season of six plays in <a href="/wiki/Repertoire_(theatre)" class="mw-redirect" title="Repertoire (theatre)">repertoire</a>, Christmas and Easter productions, and the many literature, film, mountaineering, jazz and creative arts festivals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kendal_Mountain_Festival" title="Kendal Mountain Festival">Kendal Mountain Festival</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Keswick_Mountain_Festival" title="Keswick Mountain Festival">Keswick Mountain Festival</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gastronomy">Gastronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Gastronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Lake District has been regarded as one of the best places to eat in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The region has four <a href="/wiki/Michelin_Guide" title="Michelin Guide">Michelin Star</a> restaurants: <a href="/wiki/L%27Enclume" title="L&#39;Enclume">L\'Enclume</a>, The Samling in <a href="/wiki/Ambleside" title="Ambleside">Ambleside</a>, The Forest Side and Gilpin Hotel. In addition, Cumbria has more <a href="/wiki/Microbreweries" class="mw-redirect" title="Microbreweries">microbreweries</a> than any other county in Britain and together with <a href="/wiki/Jennings_Brewery" title="Jennings Brewery">Jennings Brewery</a> supply a variety of <a href="/wiki/Ale" title="Ale">ales</a> to pubs and restaurants throughout the region.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_art">Literature and art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Literature and art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DV342_Ullswater_from_above_Pattersdale.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/DV342_Ullswater_from_above_Pattersdale.png/220px-DV342_Ullswater_from_above_Pattersdale.png" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/DV342_Ullswater_from_above_Pattersdale.png/330px-DV342_Ullswater_from_above_Pattersdale.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/DV342_Ullswater_from_above_Pattersdale.png/440px-DV342_Ullswater_from_above_Pattersdale.png 2x" data-file-width="2804" data-file-height="1664" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DV342_Ullswater_from_above_Pattersdale.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ullswater" title="Ullswater">Ullswater</a> painted by <a href="/wiki/John_Parker_(cleric)" title="John Parker (cleric)">John Parker</a> 1825</div></div></div>\n<p>The Lake District is intimately associated with <a href="/wiki/English_literature" title="English literature">English literature</a> of the 18th and 19th centuries. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gray" title="Thomas Gray">Thomas Gray</a> was the first to bring the region to attention, when he wrote a journal of his <a href="/wiki/Grand_Tour" title="Grand Tour">Grand Tour</a> in 1769, but it was <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a> whose poems were most famous and influential. Wordsworth\'s poem "<a href="/wiki/I_Wandered_Lonely_as_a_Cloud" title="I Wandered Lonely as a Cloud">I Wandered Lonely as a Cloud</a>", inspired by the sight of <a href="/wiki/Daffodils" class="mw-redirect" title="Daffodils">daffodils</a> on the shores of Ullswater, remains one of the most famous in the English language. Out of his long life of eighty years, sixty were spent amid its lakes and mountains, first as a schoolboy at <a href="/wiki/Hawkshead" title="Hawkshead">Hawkshead</a>, and afterwards living in <a href="/wiki/Grasmere" title="Grasmere">Grasmere</a> (1799\xe2\x80\x931813) and <a href="/wiki/Rydal_Mount" title="Rydal Mount">Rydal Mount</a> (1813\xe2\x80\x9350). Wordsworth, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Coleridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Southey" title="Robert Southey">Southey</a> became known as the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Poets" title="Lake Poets">Lake Poets</a>.\n</p><p>The poet and his wife lie buried in the churchyard of Grasmere and very near to them are the remains of <a href="/wiki/Hartley_Coleridge" title="Hartley Coleridge">Hartley Coleridge</a> (son of the poet <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a>), who himself lived for many years in Keswick, Ambleside and Grasmere. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Southey" title="Robert Southey">Robert Southey</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Poet_Laureate_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Poet Laureate of the United Kingdom">Poet Laureate</a> and friend of Wordsworth (who would succeed Southey as Laureate in 1843), was a resident of Keswick for forty years (1803\xe2\x80\x9343), and was buried in <a href="/wiki/Crosthwaite" title="Crosthwaite">Crosthwaite</a> churchyard. Samuel Taylor Coleridge lived for some time in Keswick, and also with the Wordsworths at Grasmere. From 1807 to 1815 <a href="/wiki/John_Wilson_(Scottish_writer)" title="John Wilson (Scottish writer)">John Wilson</a> lived at Windermere. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Quincey" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas de Quincey">Thomas de Quincey</a> spent the greater part of the years 1809 to 1828 at Grasmere, in the first cottage which Wordsworth had inhabited. Ambleside, or its environs, was also the place of residence both of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Arnold" title="Thomas Arnold">Thomas Arnold</a>, who spent there the vacations of the last ten years of his life and of <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Martineau" title="Harriet Martineau">Harriet Martineau</a>, who built herself a house there in 1845. At Keswick, Mrs Lynn Linton (wife of <a href="/wiki/William_James_Linton" title="William James Linton">William James Linton</a>) was born, in 1822. <a href="/wiki/Brantwood" title="Brantwood">Brantwood</a>, a house beside Coniston Water, was the home of <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a> during the last years of his life. His assistant <a href="/wiki/W._G._Collingwood" title="W. G. Collingwood">W. G. Collingwood</a> the author, artist and antiquarian lived nearby, and wrote <i>Thorstein of the Mere,</i> set in the Norse period.\n</p><p>In addition to these residents or natives of the Lake District, a variety of other poets and writers made visits to the Lake District or were bound by ties of friendship with those already mentioned above. These include <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Percy Bysshe Shelley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Walter_Scott" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Walter Scott">Sir Walter Scott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne" title="Nathaniel Hawthorne">Nathaniel Hawthorne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Hugh_Clough" title="Arthur Hugh Clough">Arthur Hugh Clough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Crabb_Robinson" title="Henry Crabb Robinson">Henry Crabb Robinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Sharp_(politician)" title="Richard Sharp (politician)">"Conversation" Sharp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Keats" title="John Keats">John Keats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Tennyson,_1st_Baron_Tennyson" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfred Tennyson, 1st Baron Tennyson">Lord Tennyson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Felicia_Hemans" title="Felicia Hemans">Felicia Hemans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Massey" title="Gerald Massey">Gerald Massey</a>.\n</p><p>Although it is unlikely she ever went there, <a href="/wiki/Letitia_Elizabeth_Landon" title="Letitia Elizabeth Landon">Letitia Elizabeth Landon</a> produced no less than sixteen poems on subjects within the Lake District and its surroundings, all associated with engravings within Fisher\'s Drawing Room Scrap Books, from 1832 to 1838. Also included there (1834) is <i>Grasmere Lake (A Sketch by a Cockney)</i>, a skit on becoming a \'lakes poet\'.\n</p><p>During the early 20th century, the children\'s author <a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a> was in residence at <a href="/wiki/Hill_Top,_Cumbria" title="Hill Top, Cumbria">Hill Top</a> Farm, setting many of her famous <a href="/wiki/Peter_Rabbit" title="Peter Rabbit">Peter Rabbit</a> books in the Lake District. Her life was made into a <a href="/wiki/Miss_Potter" title="Miss Potter">biopic film</a>, starring <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9e_Zellweger" title="Ren\xc3\xa9e Zellweger">Ren\xc3\xa9e Zellweger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ewan_McGregor" title="Ewan McGregor">Ewan McGregor</a>. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Ransome" title="Arthur Ransome">Arthur Ransome</a> lived in several areas of the Lake District, and set five of his <a href="/wiki/Swallows_and_Amazons_series" title="Swallows and Amazons series">Swallows and Amazons series</a> of books, published between 1930 and 1947, in a fictionalised Lake District setting. So did <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Trease" title="Geoffrey Trease">Geoffrey Trease</a> with his five Black Banner school stories (1949\xe2\x80\x9356), starting with <i><a href="/wiki/No_Boats_on_Bannermere" title="No Boats on Bannermere">No Boats on Bannermere</a></i>.\n</p><p>The novelist <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Walpole" title="Hugh Walpole">Sir Hugh Walpole</a> lived at "Brackenburn" on the lower slopes of <a href="/wiki/Catbells" class="mw-redirect" title="Catbells">Catbells</a> overlooking Derwent Water from 1924 until his death in 1941. Whilst living at "Brackenburn" he wrote <i>The Herries Chronicle</i> detailing the history of a fictional Cumbrian family over two centuries. The noted author and poet <a href="/wiki/Norman_Nicholson" title="Norman Nicholson">Norman Nicholson</a> came from the south west lakes, living and writing about <a href="/wiki/Millom" title="Millom">Millom</a> in the 20th century \xe2\x80\x93 he was known as the last of the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Poets" title="Lake Poets">Lake Poets</a> and came close to becoming the Poet Laureate.\n</p><p>The Lakes has been an inspirations for many notable artists. Some of the most famous artists to depict the region in their work have been <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Heaton_Cooper" title="Alfred Heaton Cooper">Alfred Heaton Cooper</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Heaton_Cooper" title="William Heaton Cooper">William Heaton Cooper</a>.\n</p><p>Writer and author <a href="/wiki/Melvyn_Bragg" title="Melvyn Bragg">Melvyn Bragg</a> was brought up in the region and has used it as the setting for some of his work, such as his novel <i>A Time to Dance</i>, later turned into a television drama.\n</p><p>The Lake District has been the setting for crime novels by <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Hill" title="Reginald Hill">Reginald Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Val_McDermid" title="Val McDermid">Val McDermid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Edwards_(author)" title="Martin Edwards (author)">Martin Edwards</a>. The region is also a recurring theme in <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>\'s 1926 novella <i><a href="/wiki/The_Torrents_of_Spring" title="The Torrents of Spring">The Torrents of Spring</a></i> and features prominently in <a href="/wiki/Ian_McEwan" title="Ian McEwan">Ian McEwan</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Amsterdam_(novel)" title="Amsterdam (novel)">Amsterdam</a></i>, which won the 1998 <a href="/wiki/Booker_Prize" title="Booker Prize">Booker Prize</a>. The 1996 <a href="/wiki/Eisner_Award" title="Eisner Award">Eisner Award</a> winning graphic novel <a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_One_Bad_Rat" title="The Tale of One Bad Rat">The Tale of One Bad Rat</a> by <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Talbot" title="Bryan Talbot">Bryan Talbot</a> featured a young girl\xe2\x80\x99s journey to and subsequent stay in the Lake District.\n</p><p>The Lake District is mentioned in <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice">Pride and Prejudice</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Bennet" title="Elizabeth Bennet">Elizabeth Bennet</a> looks forward to a holiday there with her aunt and uncle and is "excessively disappointed" upon learning they cannot travel that far.\n</p><p>The opening of <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Turner_Smith" title="Charlotte Turner Smith">Charlotte Turner Smith</a>\'s novel <i>Ethelinde</i> with its atmospheric description of <a href="/wiki/Grasmere" title="Grasmere">Grasmere</a> (complete with a Gothic Abbey!) is supposed to have induced Wordsworth into looking to it as a possible place of residence.\n</p><p>Film director <a href="/wiki/Ken_Russell" title="Ken Russell">Ken Russell</a> lived in the Keswick/Borrowdale area until 2007<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> and used it in films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tommy_(1975_film)" title="Tommy (1975 film)">Tommy</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mahler_(film)" title="Mahler (film)">Mahler</a></i>.\n</p><p>The German artist <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Schwitters" title="Kurt Schwitters">Kurt Schwitters</a> visited the Lake District while in exile in Great Britain and moved there permanently in June 1945, remaining there for the rest of his life.\n</p><p>The Lake District is the setting for the 1977 <a href="/wiki/Richard_Adams" title="Richard Adams">Richard Adams</a> novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Plague_Dogs" title="The Plague Dogs">The Plague Dogs</a></i>. Adams\'\nknowledge of the area offers the reader a precise view of the natural beauty of the Lake District.\n</p><p>Some students of <a href="/wiki/Arthurian" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthurian">Arthurian</a> lore identify the Lake District with the <a href="/wiki/Grail" class="mw-redirect" title="Grail">Grail</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Listeneise" title="Listeneise">Listeneise</a>.\n</p><p>The former <a href="/wiki/Keswick_School_of_Industrial_Art" title="Keswick School of Industrial Art">Keswick School of Industrial Art</a> at Keswick was started by <a href="/wiki/Canon_Rawnsley" class="mw-redirect" title="Canon Rawnsley">Canon Rawnsley</a>, a friend of <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a>.\n</p><p>Also set in the Lake District is Sophie Jackson\'s mystery novel <i>The Woman Died Thrice</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> It was published in 2016 under Jackson\'s pen name Evelyn James.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>A number of words and phrases are local to the Lake District and are part of the <a href="/wiki/Cumbrian_dialect" title="Cumbrian dialect">Cumbrian dialect</a>, though many are shared by other northern dialects. These include:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fell" title="Fell">Fell</a> \xe2\x80\x93 from <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>fjallr</i>, brought to England by <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> invaders and close to modern Norwegian <i>fjell</i> and Swedish <i>fj\xc3\xa4ll</i> meaning mountain</li>\n<li>Howe \xe2\x80\x93 place name from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>haugr</i> meaning hill, knoll, or mound</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tarn_(lake)" title="Tarn (lake)">Tarn</a> \xe2\x80\x93 a word that has been taken to mean a small lake situated in a <a href="/wiki/Cirque" title="Cirque">corrie</a> (the local name for which is <i>cove</i>), a local phrase for any small pool of water. The word is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>, Norwegian and Swedish word <i>tjern/tj\xc3\xa4rn</i>, meaning small lake.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Yan_tan_tethera" class="mw-redirect" title="Yan tan tethera">Yan tan tethera</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the name for a system of sheep counting which was traditionally used in the Lake District. Though now rare, it is still used by some and taught in local schools.</li>\n<li>Heaf (a variant of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/heft" class="extiw" title="wikt:heft">heft</a>) \xe2\x80\x93 the "home territory" of a flock of sheep</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_District&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" They contain the highest elevation in England \xe2\x80\x94 Scaw Fell (<a href="/wiki/Scafell_Pike" title="Scafell Pike">Scafell Pike</a>), 3,208 feet above the level of the sea (retrieved 2018)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-england-36905517">"Yorkshire Dales and Lake District national parks extend"</a>. 1 August 2016<span class="reference-accessdate">.
